<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">World record progression 100 metres freestyle</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_world_records_in_swimming" title="List of world records in swimming">List of world records in swimming</a></div>
<p>The first <b>world record in the men's <a href="/wiki/100_metres_freestyle" title="100 metres freestyle">100 metres freestyle</a></b> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_course" title="Long course">long course</a> (50 metres) swimming was recognised by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Swimming_Federation" title="International Swimming Federation">International Swimming Federation</a> (FINA) in 1905.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Short_course" title="Short course">short course</a> (25 metres) swimming events the world's governing body recognizes world records since 3 March 1991.</p>
<p>Times have consistently dropped over the years due to better training techniques and new developments in the sport.</p>
<p>In the first four Olympics competitions were not held in pools, but rather in open water (1896– The Mediterranean Sea, 1900– The Seine River, 1904– an artificial lake, 1906– The Mediterranean Sea). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1904_Olympics" title="1904 Olympics">1904 Olympics</a> freestyle race was the only one ever measured at 100 yards, instead of the usual 100 metres. A 100 metre pool was built for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1908_Olympics" title="1908 Olympics">1908 Olympics</a> and sat in the centre of the main stadium's track and field oval. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1912_Olympics" title="1912 Olympics">1912 Olympics</a>, held in the Stockholm harbour, marked the beginning of electronic timing.</p>
<p>Male swimmers wore full body suits up until the 1940s, which caused more drag in the water than their modern swim-wear counterparts. Also, over the years, pool designs have lessened the drag. Some design considerations allow for the reduction of swimming <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">resistance</a> making the pool faster. Namely, proper pool depth, elimination of currents, increased lane width, energy absorbing racing lane lines and gutters, and the use of other innovative hydraulic, acoustic and illumination designs.</p>
<p>In 2008, leading up to the Olympics, Speedo introduced a 50% <a href="/wiki/Polyurethane" title="Polyurethane">Polyurethane</a> suit dubbed <a href="/wiki/LZR_Racer" title="LZR Racer">LZR</a>. Pure polyurethane suits from Arena (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-Glide" title="X-Glide">X-Glide</a>), Adidas (Hydrofoil) and Italian suit manufacturer, Jaked were thought to be largely responsible for the multiple World Records in 2009 including at the <a href="/wiki/2009_World_Aquatics_Championships" title="2009 World Aquatics Championships">2009 World Aquatics Championships</a> (dubbed the "Plastic Games"). FINA announced a ban on non-textile suit that took effect on January 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/1924_Summer_Olympics" title="1924 Summer Olympics">1924 Summer Olympics</a> were the first to use the standard 50 metre pool with marked lanes. In the freestyle, swimmers originally dived from the pool walls, but diving blocks were eventually incorporated at the <a href="/wiki/1936_Summer_Olympics" title="1936 Summer Olympics">1936 Summer Olympics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tumble_turn" title="Tumble turn">tumble turn</a> ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flip-turn" title="Flip-turn">flip-turn</a>") was developed by the 1950s.</p>
